<div id="review_673373569" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/673373569" />
    <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy"><img alt="Jenny (Reading Envy)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455391853p2/68030.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/673373569?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny (Reading Envy)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny (Reading Envy)" href="/user/show/68030-jenny-reading-envy">Jenny (Reading Envy)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/5931453-the-iron-heel">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenny (Reading Envy) by: </span>
      Jesse from SFF Audio
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=sff-audio">sff-audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=activism-and-revolution">activism-and-revolution</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=read2013">read2013</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/68030-jenny-reading-envy?shelf=post-apocalypse-and-dystopia">post-apocalypse-and-dystopia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer673373569" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8120805339737721454"><i>“We are all caught up in the wheels and cogs of the industrial machine.”</i><br/><br/>When this book was selected for an SFF Audio Readalong discussion (link at bottom of post), I was surprised I hadn't heard of it in all my reading of dystopias and disasters. Jack London, an ardent socialist, published this in 1907 as a warning for the Oligarchy that was bound to take control if the proletariat didn't rise up. <br/><br/>The story itself is told through the diary of Avis Everhard, telling the story of the revolutionary</span>
  <span id="freeText8120805339737721454" style="display:none"><i>“We are all caught up in the wheels and cogs of the industrial machine.”</i><br /><br />When this book was selected for an SFF Audio Readalong discussion (link at bottom of post), I was surprised I hadn't heard of it in all my reading of dystopias and disasters. Jack London, an ardent socialist, published this in 1907 as a warning for the Oligarchy that was bound to take control if the proletariat didn't rise up. <br /><br />The story itself is told through the diary of Avis Everhard, telling the story of the revolutionary, the man she loved, and his attempts to overthrow the system. Most of the book is lectures on socialism, but there are some exciting bits of action thrown in from a far-off perspective. In an interesting twist (and this is stated at the beginning, so not really a spoiler), socialism doesn't exactly win. When we discussed the novel we puzzled over why he would tell it from that angle if he was hoping to convince people of the solution of socialism. There are even footnotes from a fictional editor to put the journal in the perspective of the current government, the "Iron Heel," which has been in power for several centuries. Knowing they don't succeed in their lifetime, or even several lifetimes, makes it a darker read than it would be otherwise.<br /><br />The Iron Heel was just too strong!<br /><br /><i>"Power. Pour it over your tongue until it tingles with it. Power."</i><br /><br />It didn't exactly go the way Jack London wrote the future, except a little over 100 years later some of his concerns seem even more alarming than ever. He also foresaw war with Germany, an attack on our ships in Hawaii, an economic crash, using war as a way to overcome financial difficulties, and other minor ideas. He was writing from a world full of natural disaster, corporate destruction, and revolution, so he just followed some of the logical conclusions through. <br /><br />One interesting note - if you search Twitter for <a href="https://twitter.com/search?q=%23ironheel" rel="nofollow noopener">#ironheel</a>, it still gets referenced fairly often. Even though this book was fairly unknown to me, it still must be read in some circles. I give it 4 stars because it was so thought provoking and made for great conversation, but as a straight novel I'd rate it more as a 3. <br /><br />ETA: <a href="http://www.sffaudio.com/?p=48910" rel="nofollow noopener">Link to podcast</a>, featuring Jesse from SFF Audio, Bryan Alexander, self-proclaimed educator, futurist, speaker, writer, and me (the only thing I proclaim is a lack of mastery). Honestly, I enjoyed the discussion more than the book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8120805339737721454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1330470865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7564604711971677239">My old 1970ish paperback has an introduction by <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1899.Howard_Zinn' title='Howard Zinn' rel='nofollow noopener'>Howard Zinn</a> that's very well done. My only prior reading of Zinn was his <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21530629.A_People_s_History_of_the_United_States_1492_Present_by_Howard_Zinn' title='A People's History of the United States 1492-Present by Howard Zinn by Howard Zinn' rel='nofollow noopener'>A People's History of the United States: 1492-Present by Howard Zinn</a>. I don't care for him too much as a historian due to his bias, but he is readable. He seems to like "Iron Heel" a lot, too.<br/><br/>My first impression was a weird one. It kind of reminds me of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/662.Atlas_Shrugged' title='Atlas Shrugged by Ayn Rand' rel='nofollow noopener'>Atlas Shrugged</a> for some reason. I don't think London & Rand could be further apart politically, though. Every comparison I ma</span>
  <span id="freeText7564604711971677239" style="display:none">My old 1970ish paperback has an introduction by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1899.Howard_Zinn" title="Howard Zinn" rel="nofollow noopener">Howard Zinn</a> that's very well done. My only prior reading of Zinn was his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/21530629.A_People_s_History_of_the_United_States_1492_Present_by_Howard_Zinn" title="A People's History of the United States 1492-Present by Howard Zinn by Howard Zinn" rel="nofollow noopener">A People's History of the United States: 1492-Present by Howard Zinn</a>. I don't care for him too much as a historian due to his bias, but he is readable. He seems to like "Iron Heel" a lot, too.<br /><br />My first impression was a weird one. It kind of reminds me of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/662.Atlas_Shrugged" title="Atlas Shrugged by Ayn Rand" rel="nofollow noopener">Atlas Shrugged</a> for some reason. I don't think London &amp; Rand could be further apart politically, though. Every comparison I make just proves out the differences, yet I still have the impression. <br /><br />The story didn't do a lot for me. I don't care for the style or anything else about it. Moving on.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1826210665" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1826210665" />
    <a title="Jose Moa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/47021487-jose-moa"><img alt="Jose Moa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546634402p2/47021487.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1826210665?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jose Moa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jose Moa" href="/user/show/47021487-jose-moa">Jose Moa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780143039716" href="/book/show/70792.The_Iron_Heel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47021487-jose-moa?shelf=utopic">utopic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47021487-jose-moa?shelf=distopian">distopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47021487-jose-moa?shelf=politics">politics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1826210665" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15711902288653308227">Jack London (1876-1940) is a polifacetic author and yet lately is mostly known by his tales in Alaska in the gold rush,some very good as To Build a Fire or Law of Life,tales of south seas and considered as young adult novels writter, as The Call of the Wild,he also have witten serious novels as for example perhaps the first postapocalliptic novel The Scarlet Fever, almost at the level of the famous The Earth Abides,and this one The Iron Heel.<br/><br/>The Iron Heel is a distopian utopian socialist novel,t</span>
  <span id="freeText15711902288653308227" style="display:none">Jack London (1876-1940) is a polifacetic author and yet lately is mostly known by his tales in Alaska in the gold rush,some very good as To Build a Fire or Law of Life,tales of south seas and considered as young adult novels writter, as The Call of the Wild,he also have witten serious novels as for example perhaps the first postapocalliptic novel The Scarlet Fever, almost at the level of the famous The Earth Abides,and this one The Iron Heel.<br /><br />The Iron Heel is a distopian utopian socialist novel,told in first person by someone that have read the manuscript finded in a oak,hidden 600 years ago that tolds the life and adventures of a socialist activist Avis Everhard and her husband Ernst Everhard executed in 1932.<br /><br />Fundamentally the novel has two parts.<br />The first is a brief clear lesson of politic economy where he explins concepts as the added value of industrial manufactures,the proces of sharing out between workers and capital of this added value ,the fight for the sharing that gives way to the fight of classes,as theworkers consum his share in prime neccesities and the capital accumulate till reach a monopolistc state,many times using practices of dumping for eliminate cmpetence.<br />In the second part ,the workers try at first take the power by democratic elections but this is showed imposible by legal or violent maneouvers of the oligarchy (here we can make a reflection over Chile and Salvador Allende case),by this the only way of take the power is violently but the monopolistic capital takes revenge by imposing a totalitarian state The Iron Heel that lasts till a underermined year but is finally defeatedand followed by a socialist utopia.<br />One has to see this novel as others similars in the histhoric context of absolute lack of rights of workers,child extenuating work and wild unregulated capitalism of the industrial revolution.<br /><br />The most interesting face of this novel is that can be considered as a anticipation novel,a fascist anticipation novel.The Iron Heel is a anticipation of all fascist regimenes in Europe.<br />But has also today truths : the servilism of mass media to the big corporations ,i have seen some sort of dumping by great food distribution corporations to exterminate little food shops, the control of courts and law and the legislative if neccesary by oligopolios,the colusion between politic class and great companys and the spining doors between politic and economic groups or lobbies.<br />The corruption of the sindicates that receive statal finantiation and by that loosing all independence, and two sort of workers:that with good salaries and a fix work in some big heavy statal or paraestatal industry corporations,this ones fierce defended by the sindicates , and those with little salaries,temporal works in minor or medium companys that are not defended or forgotten by the sindicates.<br /><br />One of the fundamental feet that joined with Looking Backwards and News from Nowhere form the triad of utopic socialist novels<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15711902288653308227" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_242434506" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/242434506" />
    <a title="wally" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4268560-wally"><img alt="wally" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1488051087p2/4268560.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/242434506?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="wally" class="user" itemprop="url" name="wally" href="/user/show/4268560-wally">wally</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6651468-the-iron-heel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4268560-wally?shelf=london">london</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer242434506" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12378079237662530546">"at first, this earth, a stage so gloomed with woe you almost sicken at the shifting of the scenes. and yet be patient. our playwright may show in some fifth act what this wild drama means."<br/><br/>contents<br/>forward<br/>1 my eagle<br/>2 challenges<br/>3 johnson's arm<br/>4 slaves of the machine<br/>5 the philomaths<br/>6 adumbrations<br/>7 the bishop's vision<br/>8 the machine breakers<br/>9 the mathematics of a dream<br/>10 the vortex<br/>11 the great adventure<br/>12 the bishop<br/>13 the general strike<br/>14 the beginning of the end<br/>15 last days<br/>16 the end<br/>17 the scarlet l</span>
  <span id="freeText12378079237662530546" style="display:none">"at first, this earth, a stage so gloomed with woe you almost sicken at the shifting of the scenes. and yet be patient. our playwright may show in some fifth act what this wild drama means."<br /><br />contents<br />forward<br />1 my eagle<br />2 challenges<br />3 johnson's arm<br />4 slaves of the machine<br />5 the philomaths<br />6 adumbrations<br />7 the bishop's vision<br />8 the machine breakers<br />9 the mathematics of a dream<br />10 the vortex<br />11 the great adventure<br />12 the bishop<br />13 the general strike<br />14 the beginning of the end<br />15 last days<br />16 the end<br />17 the scarlet livery<br />18 in the shadow of sonoma<br />19 transformation<br />20 the last oligarch<br />21 the roaring abysmal beast<br />22 the chicago commune<br />23 the people of the abyss<br />24 nightmare<br />25 the terrorists<br /><br />the iron heel/forward<br /><br />this forward is written by anthony meredith, at? ardis, november 27, 419 b.o.m. first sentence: <i>it cannot be said that the everhard manuscript is an important historical document.</i> seven centuries have passed since avis everhard completed the manuscript...the time period between 1912-1932. the manuscript is about avis's husband ernest who 'devoted his life to the revolution. everhard was one of the 1st to use the term 'iron heel' to describe the oligarchy...<i>the rise of the oligarchy will always remain a cause of secret wonder to the historian and philosopher.</i><br /><br />the manuscript was completed during the last days of preparation for the 2nd revolt (there is mention of a 3rd, 4th, 5th and so on)...avis had written the manuscript to glorify her husband...and when the revolt was failing, 'she hid the manuscript in the hollow oak at wake robin lodge,' a small bungalow in the sonoma hills of california. avis it is believed was executed.<br /><br /><b>chapter 1, my eagle</b><br />this is the beginning of the 1st person account of avis everhard, the wife of ernest. there are asterisks that are editorial commentary by persons unknown in chapter one, unless it be anthony meredith, who wrote the forward. <br /><br />avis describes the quiet before the storm. ernest her husband is dead. she expects the labor of the world to rise. as she writes, avis says that it has been two months since 'he laid down his life'. <br />they met february, 1912, at the family home in berkeley. it was 'preacher's night' at their house, as her father called it, and ernest was out of place. avis's father, john cunningham, was a professor at the state university at berkeley, physics...(sir oliver lodge)<br /><br />avis describes a prize-fighter man in ernest, while she was a product her environment, class instincts...ernest is contrasted to bishop orehouse, christ-like in appearance and goodness. ernest is bold. jesus was, as well, calling the leaders of day a den of vipers. etc and so forth.<br /><br />ernest was a superman, a blond beast such as nietzsche described. this is a hoot, hey, when was this published? 1907?<br /><br />the ministers talk of the working class and its relation to the church, etc. ernest listens. does not talk. bold? he is asked for an opinion...dr hammerfield encourages him. so, ernest tells them they know nothing about the working class. 'you are anarchists in the realm of thought.' 'you can prove everything and nothing.' 'each of you goes into his own consciousness to explain himself and the universe.'<br /><br /><b>chapter 2 challenges</b><br />in this chapter the reader learns that avis is unmarried and twenty-four. she likes ernest. telling of her father happening upon<br /> ernest addressing a crowd from a soapbox, inviting him to the house, as the father had become fired w/a passion to redress wrong. (padgett powell has a great line in mrs. hollingsworth's men about this kind of nature...it is one of my favorite quotes) ernest is at work on a book, philosophy and revolution.<br /><br />the challenges are to the bishop and to avis...ernest blames the church for enabling capitalism, for being blind to what happens to the flock, feels it should protest wrongs. ernest will show the bishop, will take him on a journey through hell, and the bishop says he will protest and ernest says he will be discharged (fired, let go).<br /><br />the challenge to avis is to give up money invested in sierra mills...a place that has caused harm, the example of mr jackson who lost an arm etc. ernest says colonel ingram, a lawyer for the mills, etc etc...avis believes there is more to the affair etc...they will see<br /><br /><b>chapter 3 jackson's arm</b><br />jackson's story of the accident...,i&gt;i'm willin' to bet that more accidents happens in the hour before whistle-blow than in all the rest of the day. jackson believed that the testimony of the foreman and the superintendent were the cause of the adverse decision of the court.<br /><br />avis visits w/jackson's lawyer...a whining fool...contrasted w/colonel ingram, corporation lawyer..."worth twenty thousand dollars a year to them." there is an asterisked note from teddy roosevelt, more or less saying that lawyers find a way for the wealthy to avoid the law.<br /><br />avis visits w/peter donnelly, one of the foremen who testified, with henry dallas, the superintendent who refused to talk, with the other foreman, james smith. they believe jackson should have gotten damages.<br />after, avis goes to her father's office in the chemistry building, encounters ernest, "our boasted civilization is based upon blood, soaked in floor, and neither your not i nor any of us can escape the scarlet stain."<br /><br />there's a bit about insurance and that scheme. ernest tells her to look up mrs wickson and mrs pertonwaithe, whose husbands are principal stockholders.<br /><br /><b>chapter 4 slaves of the machine</b><br />avis is shaken by the story of jackson's arm. she worries about ernest, wondering if he too, like christ, is destined for a cross. she meets colonel ingram...and after the initial change in him, he fell back on being a lawyer. <br /><br />next, she tries the newspapers, but they won't print anything. she got hold of percy layton, a university graduate of journalism and he explains it to her.<br /><br />she sees mr wickson and mr pertonwaithe...they believed they are right and there is a quote from john stuart mill, from <i>on liberty: wherever there is an ascendent class, a large portion of the morality emanates from is class interests and its class feelings of superiority.</i><br /><br />ernest says, "no man in the industrial machine is a free-will agent, except the large capitalist, and he isn't, if you'll pardon the irishism....one of the weaknesses of the human mind is that the wish is parent to the thought."<br /><br /><i>society was the creation of the idle rich whotoiled not and who in this way played.</i> perhaps...again, reminded of <i>mrs. hollingsworth</i>.<br /><br />carelessness is the root cause or reason for the denial of damages.<br /><br /><b>chapter 5 the philomaths</b><br />ernest was often at avis's house. "he became my oracle." this chapter is a bit of a hoot, as it reminds me of some/many of the old black and white movies, the relationships between man and woman in those--the way the man gets what he wants, he takes it. <i>his arms were around me before i knew. his lips were on mine before i could protest or resist.....he did not propose. he put his arms around me and kissed me and took it for granted that we should be married. there was no discussion about it.</i><br /><br />ernest in this chapter is like the character <i>martin eden</i>, in the novel of the same name...gentle and violent....awkward and ease...<i>his behavior in our drawing-room reminded me of a careful bull in a china shop.</i> read the first few pages of <i>martin eden</i> and you'll see what i mean. <br /><br />the philomaths: a group of people who meet once a month. no club house. london uses a neato tool here...a letter, a wrinkled letter, "written to me by ernest twenty years ago..." ernest was to receive a fee of $250 to speak. they gathered at the pertonwaithe house. 200 philomaths sat down to hear ernest.<br /><br />miss brentwood introduced him to colonel van gilbert---all the corporation lawyers have military appendages....heh! and this one is/was a will-breaker...a peculiar feature of the time. <br /><br />a dig at 'seaside library' novels, "a curious and amazzing literature that served to make the working class utterly misapprehend the nature of the leisure class."<br /><br />ernest speaks about his life...poverty, etc....the word "utopian" was used against him....and so there's this bitt about words...what's the one man say to denigrate your political opponent..."ridicule"? yeah...ridicule is the 1st response i believe..."utopian"...."the mere utterance of it could damn any scheme..." (and so it goes)<br /><br />ernest lambasts the ruling master class...there's a bitt about patent medicines...then he begins to describe the army of revolution....and this got the growls he predicted to avis...the $ threatened....there's a bitt about the census and the # in poverty....whether the number(s) is ever reported or given....<br /><br />the colonel gets some time...using ridicule..."jean jacques rosseau enunciated your socialistic theory nearly two centuries ago...." ernest and the colonel go back and forth a bit....but really, this is a vehicle for london to preach a sermon and it is a bore. there is at least one instance where the ole nugget <i>show us don't tell us</i> or some variation thereon is highly visible, and for that, we should thank london....<i>treasure the bad</i> as stephen king has said in <i>on writing</i>....on the kindle it is location 906-12....wtf kind of numbering is that? i've yet to figure it out...at the 27% read-point....footnote...okay then! i think we've all learned something here so far...recess...smoke em if you have them.<br /><br />or wait. a bit more...ernest repeats his charge: mismanagement.<br /><br />a mr wickson answers him...more or less...grapeshot...the answer to the million and a half man army that ernest spoke about. ernest ends with "it does not matter whether it is in one year, ten, or a thousand--your class shall be dragged down." the meek shall inherit the earth? i won't argue the philosophy...this is a story...but i'd hazard that ernest's time-table is less than idealistic...even if it is what...realistic...a thousand years?<br /><br />we'll see where it goes....<br /><br /><b>chapter 6 adumbrations</b><br />...warnings of coming events began to fall about us thick and fast...avis's father is getting leaned on by the upper-yucks....there is a shadow of something colossal and menacing...shadow of the oligarchy...quote from john c calhoun: a power has risen up in the government...<br /><br />bitt about the bishop...whose eyes have been opened...so...he takes in whores and beggars, and is taken himself..out of the equation. london uses the metaphor of "up in the air" for many..he uses this time and again..i believe he also uses it in <i>martin eden</i> will verify when i find it. i'm thinking <i>against the day</i><br /><br /><b>chapter 7 the bishop's vision</b><br />this is the chapter w/the bishop trying to be a christian and taken out of the equation.<br /><br /><b>chapter 8 the machine breakers</b><br />just before ernest ran for congress on socialist ticket, avis's father gives the dinner of the machine breakers as ernest called it....ernest expounds theory politikal <br /><br /><b>chapter 9 the mathematics of a dream</b><br />more expounding during the dinner of the machine breakers. (this idea, <i>breakers</i> is interesting in light of stephen king and his roland-story----breakers) has to do w/the people there wanting to break the trust, break this break that..get their profit back...more or less set them all back hundreds of years..neato, hey? tell wall street that. or they already know? i think not.<br /><br />ernest tells them they can't break the machines...there's all this theory expounded, marxist i take it, surpluses and whatnot. the death of capitalism. banks. railroads. yeehaw.<br /><br /><b>chapter 10 the vortex</b><br />avis's father is forced out of the university...he makes the most of it. a socialist paper, the appeal to reason, is forced out of business. there is a bitt about <i>the black hundreds</i>, reactionary mobs organized by the perishing autocracy in the russian revolution...echoed here. more of the same.<br /><br /><b>chapter 11 the great adventure</b><br />avis's father's wealth is stolen from him...legal theft or outright taking...home money etc. during this chapter, avis is married to ernest. they moved to san francisco, slum south of market street. ernest quotes a favorite poem..."joy upon you and gain upon gain...the unslaked thirst of an eden cursed shall harrow the earth for its fill...."<br /><br /><b>chapter 12 the bishop</b><br />avis happens to meet bishop morehouse, out and about, living the life of christ....what ernest wanted...gawd, wouldn't it be lovely to be able to ask those who clamor today, is this what you mean? surely not, for they have already placed god in the closet. topsy turvy all. in the end, they haul the bishop away to the asylum.<br /><br /><b>chapter 13, the general strike</b><br />ernest is elected to congress in a socialist landslide, as well as 49 others...grangers are elected in some dozen states, though the incumbents do not leave.<br /><br />there are some strange things happen in this chapter...prophetic? you decide. "the great german war-lord prepared, and so did the united states prepare." well, the prussian is a soldier, always had been...and the u.s.a. has never really prepared..we've gotten caught w/our pants down and out peckers in our hands. "that night a german fleet made a dash on honolulu, sinking three american cruisers and a revenue cutter...." <br /><br />next day germany and the united states declared war...but it failed since a general strike was called. did london not take into account nationalism? to think that a strike could stop war? i dunno...<br /><br /><b>chapter 14, the beginning of the end</b><br />...hmm...well...one thing in this chapter is that some big unions jump in bed w/the ruling elite...<br /><br />there's a bitt about "wonder cities"...and this anthony meredith, whose name ends the forward, lives in one, ardis..asgard is the name of another...."three centuries of the iron heel and four centuries of the brotherhood of man"...says meredith.<br /><br /><b>chapter 15 last days</b><br />grab-sharing...profit-grabbing..."arthurization" "the defection of the great unions had prevented out proletarian revolt..." in another month they send 50 men to congress....things look up, they believe.<br /><br /><b>chapter 16 the end</b><br />ernest and avis go to washington....ernest has been earning money translating...avis's father is "slumming" my word...w/an "anthropologist's" (my word) view of those around him...(as london did?)<br /><br />mucho happens...lottsa death and destruction...told, not shown...not very interesting here and for much of the story give or take a chapter or three around this point.<br /><br /><b>chapter 17 the scarlet livery</b><br />hubbub in congress...a [fake] bomb...smoke bomb...and that gets rid of all 50 socialists....avis was in the gallery that day. it'd be a real hoot to believe that wifes/husbands of representatives attend when congress is in session, wuudn't it? all 50 are placed in prisons scattered across the country....ernest is in alcatraz.<br /><br /><b>chapter 18, in the shadow of sonoma</b><br />avis spends six months in prison....there's a bitt about "fighting groups"...folk dedicated to the cause...a kind of special warfare person....there's much double-agent type stuff...people working both sides of the fence to get the goods. there's a bitt about "the passport system"....which sounds like 'papers' that all must carry.<br />some bitts &amp; pieces about members who, like james bond, both male and female, did daring deeds.<br /><br />avis is accompanied to a hideout--to the property of mr wickson, in the sonoma mountains...near the village of glen ellen.<br /><br /><b>chapter 19, transformation</b><br />ernest tells avis, via letter, that she must make herself over...to avoid capture, execution by the oligarch. there's bitts &amp; pieces about "the red virgin" and the "frisco reds"...spy-like folk, spec-war-like folk. some characters from earlier are revisited--the two colonels...jackson's lawyer is given a name here....joseph hurd...<br /><br /><b>chapter 20 a lost oligarch</b>...earlier, it says the last oligarch?<br /><br />avis becomes "mary holmes"...when ernest arrives, there is a scene played out where he does not recognize her. this, unfortunately, is not believable...we are, alas, told too much, rather than being shown this. but remember, <i>treasure the bad</i><br /><br /><b>chapter 21, the roaring abysmal beast</b><br /><i>the oligarchs had succeeded in devising a governmental machine, as intricate as it was vast, that worked--and this despite all our efforts to clog and hamper</i> this is interesting. so? what's the point? clogging for clogging's sake? let's dance?<br /><br />in a nut shell...ernest and avis in this manuscript are opposed to the idea that jackson lost his arm and wasn't taken care of...and for that reason, we need to turn the country on its head and institute new forms of gov't? getting up on the soapbox and condemning the christian for failing to take care of his fellow man didn't work, so...so what's the point?<br /><br />there's a bitt about 'mercenaries' a real interesting idea as this is something that happens now and here it happens on a large scale...could it, yes it could happen on a large scale here and now.<br /><br />...while the 'helpless mass of the population, the people of the abyss, as sinking into a brutish apathy of content w/misery.' as jefferson wrote in the declaration, all experience has shown that mankind are more disposed to suffer, while evils are sufferable, etc etc.<br /><br />there's bitts about the building of ardis, a wonder city....the building of asgard...ardis completed in 1942, asgard not until 1984.<br /><br /><b>chapter 22 the chicago commune</b><br />'ernest had largely planned the first revolt, and the date set had been somewhere early in the spring of 1918.' (this is the fall of 1917 here now) and this is another interesting piece, like the germans attacking honolulu in the earlier chapter...this is what? a year off from the russian revolution?<br /><br />chicago will be the epicenter, my word, not london's....'the people of the abyss were tormented out of their apathy.' avis and others are sent to chicago as the oligarch has learned of the plans for the revolt and will use agent-provocateur to hinder the revolt.<br /><br />lots of bloodshed.<br /><br /><b>chapter 23 the people of the abyss</b><br />pandemonium all around...some interesting scenes...skyscraper fighting....from building to building...reading it w/the benefit/hindrance of our modern age, tee-vee, etc etc...i wonder if the images i see--based on teevee images of war--are what london saw? lots of bloodshed.<br /><br /><b>chapter 24 nightmare</b><br />cont'd riot, pandemonium, bloodshed, ethnic cleansing, killing of wounded, bodies heaped together<br />avis is eventually rescued from it all. she finds herself w/ernest again.<br /><br /><b>chapter 25 the terrorists</b><br />back in new york...this chapter deals with the continued disaster that had befallen the cause with a capital c. ...there is added info from meredith, as there has been throughout the read, bitts about groups that sound like gangs...the danites...the widows of war...berserkers, valkyries....etc.<br /><br />the manuscript breaks off in the middle of a sentence...avis either fled or was captured...ernest it was known was executed...seven centuries have passed since these events described by avis in the manuscript, commented upon by meredith from ardis.<br /><br />5-stars? meh...5-stars for effort. there is some good narration here, as well as some bad...the places where london told us, instead of showing us...that 1st obvious one where ernest is arguing with the other and the other side is not really given voice...we're told he said this or that, but the colonel does not say it...we are told...we are not shown.<br /><br />as the other place mentioned above, told not shown.<br /><br />there are large sections of preaching, ernest/london telling the world what's wrong with the world, the answer, socialism. and what would be the point of arguing the story? that is what ernest believed, what, i take it? that london believed? or a fraction of it? perhaps.<br /><br />and i am out of room!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12378079237662530546" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText14701084522791829380" style="display:none">I’ve often wondered if Jack London was a stranded time traveler from a parallel dystopian future and this book proves it. The format is surprisingly similar to “The Handmaids Tale” which of course was written 80 years after this book. <br />The plot involves the Oligarchy (1%ers vs. Socalists) modern Social Democrats come to mind. Instead of the Japanese attacking Pearl Harbor the Germans launch a surprise attack on Honolulu. <br />London was a straight up Socalist (workers seize the control factories) which still might be a bridge to far for the modern reader. But, His warnings about income inequality and the death of the middle class ring true in our current times.</span>

<div id="review_2280636556" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2280636556" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2280636556?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781546470618" href="/book/show/37009549-the-iron-heel">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=leftwing-polemical">leftwing-polemical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=loves-big-brother">loves-big-brother</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=fascists-wtf">fascists-wtf</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2280636556" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16431067062652696414">An outline of Trump Plan No. 2 (No. 1 is Lewis’ <i>It Can’t Happen Here</i>).<br/><br/>Presented as a found manuscript by a faux editor, “seven centuries” after the events it describes (2), this text describes fascistic irruption as “a step aside, or a step backward, to the social tyrannies that made the early world a hell, but that were as necessary as the Iron Heel was unnecessary” (3). That is, “capitalism, rotten-ripe, sent forth that monstrous offshoot, the Oligarchy” (4). <br/><br/>The text, written in 1908, has som</span>
  <span id="freeText16431067062652696414" style="display:none">An outline of Trump Plan No. 2 (No. 1 is Lewis’ <i>It Can’t Happen Here</i>).<br /><br />Presented as a found manuscript by a faux editor, “seven centuries” after the events it describes (2), this text describes fascistic irruption as “a step aside, or a step backward, to the social tyrannies that made the early world a hell, but that were as necessary as the Iron Heel was unnecessary” (3). That is, “capitalism, rotten-ripe, sent forth that monstrous offshoot, the Oligarchy” (4). <br /><br />The text, written in 1908, has some cool colloquies regarding leftwing ideas, such as the debate about whether ‘class struggle’ is the same as ‘class hatred’ (19 ff); it is not, of course, even though the deliberately indifferent brand of liberals and everyone to their right doesn’t draw the distinction.<br /><br />The story sets forth initially a dialectic among opponents of the capitalist order as between saboteur primitivists (72 ff) and progressive expropriators (75 ff). Marx eventually comes up (84). In discussing a generalized movement, they are confronted with the stark potential of Agamben’s state of exception: “in that day when you speak of rising in your strength, your strength would be turned against yourself” (77), laying out the control of militias and courts by the owners.<br /><br />The narrator finds it “monstrous, impossible, that our whole society was based upon blood” (36), after a meritorious workers’ compensation claim is crushed by capital. Underlying this bloody basis is the “master ethic” of the owners (40), who “talked about policy and right” but also “believed absolutely that their conduct was right,” “convinced that they were the saviours of society” (id.). This section is footnoted with a comment from J.S. Mill: “Wherever there is an ascendant class, a large portion of the morality emanates from its class interests and its class feelings of superiority,” which is just a variation on the Marxist principle that the ruling ideas of every era are the ideas of the ruling class, absent the chiasmus. This is easily accomplished when the owners have the “the power of subsidization of thought” (41). The faux editor notes that these owners nevertheless are “gilded drones that did no labor, but only glutted themselves at the honey-vats of the workers” (42).<br /><br />But “history was making fast” (96). The narrator’s father writes an academic study of how capital controls education, which the cappies censor--and this coincides with an economic crisis, a large strike, and other conflict, including partisan violence: “All cities and towns were like armed camps, and laborers were shot down like dogs” (98); the lumpenproles are recruited by capital for antisocial tasks: “Out of the vast army of the unemployed the strikebreakers were recruited; and when the strikebreakers were worsted by the labor unions, the troops always appeared and crushed the unions” (id.)—stories familiar to readers of Zinn regarding the late 19th century. After this, it spirals out of control in many countries, wherein lumpenized mobs become Stormtroopers in the service of capital, after which “the Iron Heel had triumphed in open warfare, but we held our own in the new warfare” (139), referring to espionage, infiltration, subversion. Open warfare starts and stops; there is a resolution, but only contained in the editorial notes from centuries later. The crucial debate during the period of the fascistic regime is how the oligarchy might maintain authority, despite ruling from a state of exception: some “ascribed the strength of the Iron Heel to its system of reward and punishment” (169)—but the narrator disagrees insofar as “the great driving force of the oligarchs is the belief that they are doing right” (id.). At times the text seems proto-althusserian in describing the methods.<br /><br />The intellectual climax, for me, is an early political discussion, wherein a left revolutionary makes the pragmatic argument that the “capitalist class has mismanaged society” and therefore should be dispossessed (55). Some cappies try to argue, but lose, of course. The last resort of property is naturally fascism: <blockquote>This, then, is our answer. We have no words to waste on you. When you reach out your vaunted strong hands for our palaces and purpled ease, we will show you what strength is. In the roar of shell and shrapnel and in whine of machine guns will our answer be couched. We will grind you revolutionists down under our heel, and we shall walk upon your faces. The world is ours, we are its lords, and ours it shall remain. (56)</blockquote> Recommended for those who ought not be selfish but will continue to be selfish as long as they live in a system based on pig-ethics, readers who sneer at charity as a poulticing of an ulcer, and people of the abyss.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16431067062652696414" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1377176748" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1377176748" />
    <a title="Shane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1798222-shane"><img alt="Shane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1526065583p2/1798222.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1377176748?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shane" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shane" href="/user/show/1798222-shane">Shane</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1377176748" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer347987588465934347">A dystopian novel, the precursor to Orwell’s 1984, that has echoes of today’s ascendance of the “One Percent,” or the Oligarchy as London called it during his day.<br/><br/>The device used to convey the story is an interesting one: a historical record kept by the wife, Avis Everhard, of the founder of the Revolution, Ernest Everhard, of an uprising that raged against the Oligarchy for 300 years before Socialism finally took hold in the form of the Brotherhood of Man (BOM); this manuscript has been found 7</span>
  <span id="freeText347987588465934347" style="display:none">A dystopian novel, the precursor to Orwell’s 1984, that has echoes of today’s ascendance of the “One Percent,” or the Oligarchy as London called it during his day.<br /><br />The device used to convey the story is an interesting one: a historical record kept by the wife, Avis Everhard, of the founder of the Revolution, Ernest Everhard, of an uprising that raged against the Oligarchy for 300 years before Socialism finally took hold in the form of the Brotherhood of Man (BOM); this manuscript has been found 700 years after it was written in the early 1900s, and 400 years into the development of BOM. The curator of the manuscript, one Anthony Meredith, is able to add footnotes to explain historical details that have occurred between then and now (circa 2600AD) without taking away from the pace of the main story line. Anthony also gives us glimpses of his world of BOM where there are no more strikes, no quarrels, no cream or butter (what do they eat?), no pedlars, no thievery, no poor housing, no bankruptcies, no corporate lawyers, and no insurance - a veritable utopia after years of struggle by the proletariat.<br /><br />Despite the enormous wealth that Jack London garnered in his short life as a writer, he was an avowed socialist, and this novel, written towards the end of his life, is his manifesto, and it reads like one, for the book is full of speeches (Ernest is a stand-in for Jack) and the narrative creates a horrifying picture of a dystopia without telling a story as a novel should. Political ideologies are bandied about blatantly. The premise is simply that the Oligarchy is slowly gaining ascendance in the early 1900’s (isn’t that true of today as well?) at the expense of the free market, and is amassing surpluses while the proletariat is getting poorer. By using its vast financial resources, the Oligarchy divides and conquers by funding professionals, trade unions, the military, and other organizations that are vital to its growth, at the expense of the downtrodden masses. The philosophy of the Oligarchy is “Combination is better than Competition.” The Socialists, who are pitted against the Oligarchy, espouse “Meeting Combination with Greater Combination.” The mounting confrontation between the Oligarchy and the Socialists finally breaks out into open warfare that lasts 300 years, but we are spared the whole episode. We just get a taste of the Revolution’s start in 1913 and its first few years when the Socialists are on the defensive, and the narrative ends in mid sentence (another good device to show the authenticity of Avis’s manuscript) as if its writer had to suddenly drop everything and run into hiding. <br /><br />There are some flaws in London’s plot. I fail to see how the Oligarchy could wipe out the middle class at home and abroad and survive if there is no one left to buy it’s “surpluses.” “Greed” is another un-factored element in the Socialist cause; just as it led to the downfall of various socialist regimes in the 1990’s and fueled countless boom-bust cycles in the capitalist stock market, greed is also likely penetrate BOM at some point and lead to its eventual downfall.<br /><br />The characters come across as wooden and we don’t really get a sense of how they feel even though we know how they think. And the adulation that Avis showers upon her husband is a bit nauseating at times - did London seek such reverence? And yet the final scene, the battle for Chicago is well painted from Avis’s viewpoint, where chaos reigns and it is hard to say who is winning or losing, all that is evident is the destruction of war that leaves its cruel mark all over the landscape. It is left to Anthony Meredith, 700 years later, to shed light on what really happened during that seminal event and how it served as the genesis for a 300-year struggle of many such uprisings before the evil Oligarchy was finally brought to heel.<br /><br />A different kind of London book, not one capturing his experiences in the wild, but one showcasing his beliefs about the world and its future. We, of course, do not have to agree with him, although he pushes some buttons that make us stop and think.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="347987588465934347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_550179360" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/550179360" />
    <a title="Edie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5367871-edie"><img alt="Edie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1304522300p2/5367871.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/550179360?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edie" href="/user/show/5367871-edie">Edie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer550179360" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14467185914854556484">I give this book 5 stars for being revolutionary, in more ways than one. When it was written, I think the closest there was to the dystopian genre was H.G. Well's Time Machine. It was a leader in that sense, but it was also incredibly predictive and insightful to many future events. I had to stop reading several times to check the original copyright of the book. Was it really only written in 1908? How on earth did Jack London, the author best known for books like Call of the Wild and White Fang,</span>
  <span id="freeText14467185914854556484" style="display:none">I give this book 5 stars for being revolutionary, in more ways than one. When it was written, I think the closest there was to the dystopian genre was H.G. Well's Time Machine. It was a leader in that sense, but it was also incredibly predictive and insightful to many future events. I had to stop reading several times to check the original copyright of the book. Was it really only written in 1908? How on earth did Jack London, the author best known for books like Call of the Wild and White Fang, with frightening accuracy, predict the events of WWI, the Japanese military aggresive society, and more?<br /><br />Now, I'm not a Socialist, and I don't consider myself very political in general. So when this book began to sound more and more like Socialist propoganda, I was a little turned off. But I still rate this 5 stars for the way the author expressed his political views. I'd never heard things described so simplistically and logically. It was very impressive.<br /><br />I was also impressed by the creative format of the book, which is penned several centuries in the future (2600 A.D.) by scholar Anthony Meredith. The narrator (Meredith) reads to us from the "Everhard Manuscript", which was written by Avis Everhard who lived during the turn of the 20th century. The manuscript covers the period of 1912 through 1932 and centers around Avis' experiences with the young revolutionary Ernest Everhard.<br /><br />I don't want to give too much of the novel away, because I really think that a lot of my friends would enjoy this book. It's an easy compelling read and it's oddly relavent to today's political atmosphere.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14467185914854556484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8676555445496870023">This was probably the first overtly anti-Liberal (economics) pro-Socialist writing I read, so I was enthralled as I imagine any remotely-decent person would be. <br/>Now looking back, I acknowledge the blunt delivery and clumsy story-line. Still, I'd pick this over the heap of "literary masterpieces" with garbage ideologies.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('R/Lv/AUPJfi6bffmxe/GqCcq6yuV3Ukh7GKXTl5QObLq5cCbceADw9wXIurZNugjQ60OQRfm8bbaObBYS8x3Sw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('3YzhCVP5hK945nvu7stENqmnwz4j4Fi+/WXm6ltNnMJwm85uJxailB6cruLyEmq9zSAmVKHb4CnLPsH8TtHSOw==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1240.Jack_London">About Jack London</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1240.Jack_London"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1508674808p3/1240.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/1240.Jack_London">Jack London
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,323
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11204284141498507322">Jack London was an American novelist, journalist, social-activist and short-story writer whose works deal romantically with elemental struggles for survival. At his peak, he was the highest paid and the most popular of all living writers. Because of early financial difficulties, he was largely self educated past grammar school.<br/><br/>London drew heavily on his life experiences in his writing. He spent ti</span>
  <span id="freeText11204284141498507322" style="display:none">Jack London was an American novelist, journalist, social-activist and short-story writer whose works deal romantically with elemental struggles for survival. At his peak, he was the highest paid and the most popular of all living writers. Because of early financial difficulties, he was largely self educated past grammar school.<br /><br />London drew heavily on his life experiences in his writing. He spent time in the Klondike during the Gold Rush and at various times was an oyster pirate, a seaman, a sealer, and a hobo. His first work was published in 1898. From there he went on to write such American classics as <i>Call of the Wild, Sea Wolf</i>, and <i>White Fang</i>.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11204284141498507322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

            <span class="readable">&ldquo;I know nothing that I may say can influence you," he said. "You have no souls to be influenced. You are spineless, flaccid things. You pompously call yourselves Republicans and Democrats. There is no Republican Party. There is no Democratic Party. There are no Republicans nor Democrats in this House. You are lick-spittlers and panderers, the creatures of the Plutocracy.&rdquo;</span>
